       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="418" height="306" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg/200px-Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg/300px-Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg/400px-Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Persepolis_Apadana_noerdliche_Treppe_Detail.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Apadana Palace in
         <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">
          Persepolis
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">
          Iran
         </a>
         , northern stairway (detail) – fifth-century BC
         <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
          Achaemenid
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">
          bas-relief
         </a>
         shows a Mede soldier in traditional Mede costume (behind Persian soldier)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        Medes
       </b>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [N 1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="'m' in 'my'">
            m
           </span>
           <span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">
            iː
           </span>
           <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
            d
           </span>
           <span title="'z' in 'zoom'">
            z
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Persian_language" title="Old Persian language">
        Old Persian
       </a>
       <i>
        <span lang="peo-Latn" xml:lang="peo-Latn">
         Māda-
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">
        Ancient Greek
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        Μῆδοι
       </span>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
        Hebrew
       </a>
       :
       <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Keter Aram Tsova', 'Taamey Ashkenaz', 'Taamey David CLM', 'Taamey Frank CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Keter YG', 'Shofar', 'David CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', 'Simple CLM', 'Nachlieli', 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', Cardo, Alef, 'Noto Serif Hebrew', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', 'David Libre', David, 'Times New Roman', Gisha, Arial, FreeSerif, FreeSans;">
        מָדַי
       </span>
       ‬) were an
       <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">
        ancient Iranian people
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [N 2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       who lived in an area known as
       <a href="/wiki/Media_(region)" title="Media (region)">
        Media
       </a>
       (northwestern
       <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">
        Iran
       </a>
       ) and who spoke the
       <a href="/wiki/Median_language" title="Median language">
        Median language
       </a>
       . At around 1100 to 1000 BC, they inhabited the mountainous area of northwestern Iran and the northeastern and eastern region of
       <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">
        Mesopotamia
       </a>
       and located in the
       <a href="/wiki/Hamadan" title="Hamadan">
        Hamadan
       </a>
       (Ecbatana) region.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia_Judaica_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia_Judaica-7">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Their emergence in Iran is thought to have occurred between 800 BC and 700 BC, and in the 7th century the whole of western Iran and some other territories were under Median rule. Its precise geographical extent remains unknown.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A few archaeological sites (discovered in the "
       <a href="/wiki/Medes#Archaeological_evidence" title="Medes">
        Median triangle
       </a>
       " in western Iran) and textual sources (from contemporary
       <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">
        Assyrians
       </a>
       and also
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
        ancient Greeks
       </a>
       in later centuries) provide a brief documentation of the history and culture of the Median state. Apart from a few personal names, the language of the Medes is unknown. The Medes had an ancient Iranian religion (a form of pre-
       <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">
        Zoroastrian
       </a>
       Mazdaism or
       <a href="/wiki/Mithra" title="Mithra">
        Mithra
       </a>
       worshipping) with a priesthood named as "
       <a href="/wiki/Magi" title="Magi">
        Magi
       </a>
       ". Later during the reigns of the last Median kings, the reforms of
       <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">
        Zoroaster
       </a>
       spread into western Iran.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cities_and_tribes_of_Media">
        Cities and tribes of Media
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Medes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Cities and tribes of Media">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Besides Ecbatana (modern
       <a href="/wiki/Hamadan" title="Hamadan">
        Hamadan
       </a>
       ), the other cities existing in Media were
       <a href="/wiki/Laodicea_(Mesopotamia)" title="Laodicea (Mesopotamia)">
        Laodicea
       </a>
       (modern
       <a href="/wiki/Nahavand" title="Nahavand">
        Nahavand
       </a>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the mound that was the largest city of the Medes, Rhages (present-day
       <a href="/wiki/Rey,_Iran" title="Rey, Iran">
        Rey
       </a>
       ). The fourth city of Media was
       <a href="/wiki/Apamea_(Media)" title="Apamea (Media)">
        Apamea
       </a>
       , near
       <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">
        Ecbatana
       </a>
       , whose precise location is now unknown. In later periods, Medes and especially Mede soldiers are identified and portrayed prominently in ancient archaeological sites such as
       <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">
        Persepolis
       </a>
       , where they are shown to have a major role and presence in the military of the
       <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
        Achaemenid Empire
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       According to the
       <a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">
        <i>
         Histories
        </i>
        of Herodotus
       </a>
       , there were six Median tribes:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Thus
        <a href="/wiki/Deioces" title="Deioces">
         Deioces
        </a>
        collected the Medes into a nation, and ruled over them alone. Now these are the tribes of which they consist: the
        <i>
         Busae
        </i>
        , the
        <i>
         Paretaceni
        </i>
        , the
        <i>
         Struchates
        </i>
        , the
        <i>
         Arizanti
        </i>
        , the
        <i>
         Budii
        </i>
        , and the
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Magi" title="Magi">
          Magi
         </a>
        </i>
        .
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       The six Median tribes resided in Media proper, the triangular shaped area between Ecbatana, Rhagae and Aspadana.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.no_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-books.google.no-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In modern
       <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">
        Iran
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       that is the area between
       <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">
        Tehran
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">
        Isfahan
       </a>
       and Hamadan. Of the Median tribes, the Magi resided in Rhaga,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       modern
       <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">
        Tehran
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was a type of sacred caste, which ministered to the spiritual needs of the Medes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Paretaceni tribe resided in and around Aspadana, modern
       <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">
        Isfahan
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.no_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-books.google.no-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the Arizanti lived in and around
       <a href="/wiki/Kashan" title="Kashan">
        Kashan
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.no_11-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-books.google.no-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the Busae tribe lived in and around the future Median capital of Ecbatana.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.no_11-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-books.google.no-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Struchates and the Budii lived in villages in the Median triangle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">
        Etymology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Medes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The original source for different words used to call the Median people, their language and homeland is a directly transmitted Old Iranian geographical name which is attested as the Old Persian "Māda-" (
       <small>
        sing. masc.
       </small>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tavernier_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tavernier-19">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The meaning of this word is not precisely established.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tavernier_19-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tavernier-19">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The linguist
       <a href="/wiki/Wojciech_Skalmowski" title="Wojciech Skalmowski">
        W. Skalmowski
       </a>
       proposes a relation with the proto-Indo European word "med(h)-" meaning "central, suited in the middle" by referring to Old Indic "madhya-" and Old Iranian "maidiia-" both carrying the same meaning
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tavernier_19-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tavernier-19">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and having descendants including Latin
       <i>
        medium
       </i>
       , Greek
       <i>
        méso
       </i>
       , and German
       <i>
        mittel
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The Median people are mentioned by that name in many ancient texts. According to the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">
         Histories of Herodotus
        </a>
       </i>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herodotus-vii-62_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Herodotus-vii-62-21">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        The Medes were called anciently by all people Aryans; but when
        <a href="/wiki/Medea" title="Medea">
         Medea
        </a>
        , the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colchian" title="Colchian">
         Colchian
        </a>
        , came to them from
        <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">
         Athens
        </a>
        , they changed their name. Such is the account which they themselves give.
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_geography_of_Media">
        Historical geography of Media
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Medes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Historical geography of Media">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The original population area of the Median people was Northwest Iran and named after them as "Media". At the end of the 2nd millennium BC the Median tribes emerged in the region (one of several
       <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">
        Iranian
       </a>
       tribes to do so) which they later called Media. These tribes expanded their control over larger areas. Subsequently, over a period of several hundred years, the boundaries of Media changed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_textual_sources">
        Ancient textual sources
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Medes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ancient textual sources">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       An early description of the territory of Media by the Assyrians dates from the end of the 9th century BC until the beginning of the 7th century BC. The southern border of Media, in that period, is named as the Elamite region of
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sima%C5%A1ki&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Simaški (page does not exist)">
        Simaški
       </a>
       in present-day
       <a href="/wiki/Lorestan_Province" title="Lorestan Province">
        Lorestan Province
       </a>
       . To the west and northwest it was bounded by the
       <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">
        Zagros Mountains
       </a>
       and from the east by
       <a href="/wiki/Dasht-e_Kavir" title="Dasht-e Kavir">
        Dasht-e Kavir
       </a>
       . This region of Media was ruled by the Assyrians and for them the region "extended along the Great Khorasan Road from just east of Harhar to Alwand, and probably beyond. It was limited on the north by the non Iranian state of the
       <a href="/wiki/Mannaeans" title="Mannaeans">
        Mannaeans
       </a>
       , on the south by
       <a href="/wiki/Ellipi" title="Ellipi">
        Ellipi
       </a>
       ."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The location of
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Harhar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Harhar (page does not exist)">
        Harhar
       </a>
       is suggested to be "the central or eastern"
       <a href="/wiki/Mahidasht_District" title="Mahidasht District">
        Mahidasht District
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Kermanshah_Province" title="Kermanshah Province">
        Kermanshah Province
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On the east and southeast of Media, as described by the Assyrians, another land with the name of "Patušarra" appears. This land was located near a mountain range which the Assyrians call "Bikni" and describe as "Lapis Lazuli Mountain". There are various opinion on the location of this mountain.
       <a href="/wiki/Mount_Damavand" title="Mount Damavand">
        Mount Damavand
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">
        Tehran
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Alvand" title="Alvand">
        Alvand
       </a>
       of Hamadan are two proposed sites. This location is the most remote eastern area that the Assyrians knew of or reached during their expansion until the beginning of the 7th century BC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the sources from Achaemenid Iran and specifically from the
       <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">
        Behistun Inscription
       </a>
       (2.76, 77–78) the capital of Media is named as "Hamgmatāna-" in Old Persian (and as Elamite
       <i>
        Agmadana
       </i>
       -, Babylonian
       <i>
        Agamtanu-
       </i>
       , etc.). The classical authors transmitted this as
       <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">
        Ecbatana
       </a>
       . This site is modern Hamadan province.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeological_evidence">
        Archaeological evidence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Medes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Archaeological evidence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ecbatane_-_excavated_house.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="667" data-file-width="1024" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Ecbatane_-_excavated_house.jpg/200px-Ecbatane_-_excavated_house.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Ecbatane_-_excavated_house.jpg/300px-Ecbatane_-_excavated_house.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Ecbatane_-_excavated_house.jpg/400px-Ecbatane_-_excavated_house.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ecbatane_-_excavated_house.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Excavation from ancient Ecbatane, Hamadan, Iran
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Median archaeological sources are rare. The discoveries of Median sites happened only after the 1960s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stronach-EIr-288_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-stronach-EIr-288-27">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For 1960 the search for Median archeological sources has mostly focused in an area known as the “Median triangle,” defined roughly as the region bounded by Hamadān, Malāyer (in Hamdan province) and Kangāvar (in Kermanshah province).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stronach-EIr-288_27-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-stronach-EIr-288-27">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Three major sites from central western Iran in the
       <a href="/wiki/Three-age_system" title="Three-age system">
        Iron Age III period
       </a>
       (i.e. 850–500 BC) are
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young-p449_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Young-p449-28">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Noushijan_Tappe" title="Noushijan Tappe">
         Tepe Nush-i Jan
        </a>
        (a primarily religious site of Median period),
       </li>
      </ul>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        The site is located 14 km west of Malāyer in Hamadan province.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stronach-EIr-288_27-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-stronach-EIr-288-27">
          [25]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The excavations started in 1967 with
        <a href="/wiki/David_Stronach" title="David Stronach">
         D. Stronach
        </a>
        as the director.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
         <a href="#cite_note-29">
          [27]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The remains of four main buildings in the site are "the central temple, the western temple, the fort, and the columned hall" which according to Stronach were likely to have been built in the order named and predate the latter occupation of the first half of the 6th century BC.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stronach-EIr-290_30-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-stronach-EIr-290-30">
          [28]
         </a>
        </sup>
        According to Stronach, the central temple, with its stark design, "provides a notable, if mute, expression of religious belief and practice".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stronach-EIr-290_30-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-stronach-EIr-290-30">
          [28]
         </a>
        </sup>
        A number of ceramics from the Median levels at Tepe Nush-i Jan have been found which are associated with a period (the second half of the 7th century BC) of power consolidation in the Hamadān areas. These findings show four different wares known as “common ware” (buff, cream, or light red in colour and with gold or silver mica temper) including jars in various size the largest of which is a form of ribbed
        <a href="/wiki/Pithos" title="Pithos">
         pithoi
        </a>
        . Smaller and more elaborate vessels were in “grey ware”, (these display smoothed and burnished surface). The “cooking ware” and “crumbly ware” are also recognized each in single handmade products.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stronach-EIr-290_30-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-stronach-EIr-290-30">
          [28]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Godin_Tepe" title="Godin Tepe">
         Godin Tepe
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Godin_Tepe#Level_II" title="Godin Tepe">
         its period II
        </a>
        : a fortified palace of a Median king or tribal chief),
       </li>
      </ul>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        The site is located 13 km east of Kangāvar city on the left bank of the river Gamas Āb". The excavations, started in 1965, were led by T. C. Young, Jr. which according to
        <a href="/wiki/David_Stronach" title="David Stronach">
         David Stronach
        </a>
        , evidently shows an important
        <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">
         Bronze Age
        </a>
        construction that was reoccupied sometime before the beginning of the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_age" title="Iron age">
         Iron III
        </a>
        period. The excavations of Young indicate the remains of part of a single residence of a local ruler which later became quite substantial.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stronach-EIr-288_27-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-stronach-EIr-288-27">
          [25]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This is similar to those mentioned often in Assyrian sources.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young-p449_28-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Young-p449-28">
          [26]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Baba_Jan,_East_Azerbaijan" title="Baba Jan, East Azerbaijan">
         Baba Jan, East Azerbaijan
        </a>
        (probably the seat of a lesser tribal ruler of Media).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        The site is located in northeastern Luristan with a distance of roughly 10 km from Nūrābād in Lurestan province. The excavations were conducted by C. Goff in 1966–69. The second level of this site probably dates to the 7th century BC.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
         <a href="#cite_note-31">
          [29]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       These sources have both similarities (in cultural characteristics) and differences (due to functional differences and diversity among the Median tribes).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young-p449_28-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Young-p449-28">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The architecture of these archaeological findings, that can probably be dated to the Median period, show a link between the tradition of columned audience halls often seen in Achaemenid (for example in Persepolis) and Safavid Iran (for example in "
       <a href="/wiki/Chehel_Sotoun" title="Chehel Sotoun">
        Chehel Sotoun
       </a>
       " from the 17th century AD) and the Median architecture.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young-p449_28-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Young-p449-28">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The materials found at Tepe Nush-i Jan, Godin Tepe, and other sites located in Media together with the Assyrian reliefs show the existence of urban settlements in Media in the first half of the 1st millennium BC which had functioned as centres for the production of handicrafts and also of an agricultural and cattle-breeding economy of a secondary type.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For other historical documentation, the archaeological evidence, though rare, together with cuneiform records by Assyrian make it possible, regardless of Herodotus' accounts, to establish some of the early history of Medians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Medes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_to_power_and_fall">
        Rise to power and fall
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Medes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Rise to power and fall">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pre-Achaemenid_Era.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1035" data-file-width="1359" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Pre-Achaemenid_Era.gif/350px-Pre-Achaemenid_Era.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Pre-Achaemenid_Era.gif/525px-Pre-Achaemenid_Era.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Pre-Achaemenid_Era.gif/700px-Pre-Achaemenid_Era.gif 2x" width="350"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pre-Achaemenid_Era.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Timeline of Pre-Achaemenid era.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rhyton_media.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Rhyton_media.jpg/250px-Rhyton_media.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Rhyton_media.jpg/375px-Rhyton_media.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Rhyton_media.jpg/500px-Rhyton_media.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rhyton_media.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Rhyton" title="Rhyton">
          Rhyton
         </a>
         in the shape of a ram's head, gold – western Iran – Median, late 7th–early 6th century BC
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">
        Iranian
       </a>
       tribes were present in western and northwestern Iran from at least the 12th or 11th centuries BC. But the significance of Iranian elements in these regions were established from the beginning of the second half of the 8th century BC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dandamaev-lukonin-2-3_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-dandamaev-lukonin-2-3-34">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By this time the Iranian tribes were the majority in what later become the territory of the Median kingdom and also the west of
       <a href="/wiki/Medes#Historical_geography_of_Media" title="Medes">
        Media proper
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dandamaev-lukonin-2-3_34-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-dandamaev-lukonin-2-3-34">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A study of textual sources from the region shows that in the Neo-Assyrian period, the regions of Media, and further to the west and the northwest, had a population with Iranian speaking people as the majority.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This period of migration coincided with a power vacuum in the
       <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">
        Near East
       </a>
       with the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Assyrian_Empire" title="Middle Assyrian Empire">
        Middle Assyrian Empire
       </a>
       (1365–1020 BC), which had dominated northwestern Iran and eastern
       <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">
        Anatolia
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">
        Caucasus
       </a>
       , going into a comparative decline. This allowed new peoples to pass through and settle. In addition
       <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">
        Elam
       </a>
       , the dominant power in Iran, was suffering a period of severe weakness, as was
       <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">
        Babylonia
       </a>
       to the west.
      </p>
      <p>
       In western and northwestern Iran and in areas further west prior to Median rule, there is evidence of the earlier political activity of the powerful societies of Elam, Mannaea,
       <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">
        Assyria
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">
        Urartu
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dandamaev-lukonin-2-3_34-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-dandamaev-lukonin-2-3-34">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There are various and up-dated opinions on the positions and activities of Iranian tribes in these societies and prior to the "major Iranian state formations" in the late 7th century BC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dandamaev-lukonin-2-3_34-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-dandamaev-lukonin-2-3-34">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One opinion (of Herzfeld,
       <i>
        et al.
       </i>
       ) is that the ruling class were "Iranian migrants" but the society was "autonomous" while another opinion (of Grantovsky,
       <i>
        et al.
       </i>
       ) holds that both the ruling class and basic elements of the population were Iranian.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       From the 10th to the late 7th centuries BC, the western parts of Media fell under the domination of the vast
       <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">
        Neo-Assyrian Empire
       </a>
       based in northern
       <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">
        Mesopotamia
       </a>
       , which stretched from
       <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
        Cyprus
       </a>
       to Iran, and from the
       <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">
        Caucasus
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">
        Egypt
       </a>
       and the north of the
       <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">
        Arabian Peninsula
       </a>
       . Assyrian kings such as
       <a href="/wiki/Tiglath-Pileser_III" title="Tiglath-Pileser III">
        Tiglath-Pileser III
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sargon_II" title="Sargon II">
        Sargon II
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sennacherib" title="Sennacherib">
        Sennacherib
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Esarhaddon" title="Esarhaddon">
        Esarhaddon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ashurbanipal" title="Ashurbanipal">
        Ashurbanipal
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ashur-etil-ilani" title="Ashur-etil-ilani">
        Ashur-etil-ilani
       </a>
       imposed
       <i>
        Vassal Treaties
       </i>
       upon the Median rulers, and also protected them from predatory raids by marauding
       <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">
        Scythians
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">
        Cimmerians
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the reign of
       <a href="/wiki/Sinsharishkun" title="Sinsharishkun">
        Sinsharishkun
       </a>
       (622–612 BC) the Assyrian empire, which had been in a state of constant civil war since 626 BC, began to unravel. Subject peoples, such as the Medes, Babylonians,
       <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">
        Chaldeans
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">
        Egyptians
       </a>
       , Scythians, Cimmerians,
       <a href="/wiki/Lydians" title="Lydians">
        Lydians
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">
        Arameans
       </a>
       quietly ceased to pay tribute to Assyria.
      </p>
      <p>
       Neo-Assyrian dominance over the Medians came to an end during the reign of
       <a href="/wiki/Cyaxares" title="Cyaxares">
        Cyaxares
       </a>
       , who in alliance with
       <a href="/wiki/Nabopolassar" title="Nabopolassar">
        Nabopolassar
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">
        Babylon
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">
        Chaldea
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">
        Scythians
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">
        Cimmerians
       </a>
       , attacked and destroyed the strife riven empire between 616 and 605 BC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The newfound alliance helped the Medes to capture
       <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">
        Nineveh
       </a>
       in 612 BC, which resulted in the eventual collapse of the Neo-Assyrian Empire by 605 BC. The Medes were subsequently able to establish their Median kingdom (with
       <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">
        Ecbatana
       </a>
       as their royal centre) beyond their original homeland and had eventually a territory stretching roughly from northeastern Iran to the
       <a href="/wiki/K%C4%B1z%C4%B1l%C4%B1rmak_River" title="Kızılırmak River">
        Kızılırmak River
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">
        Anatolia
       </a>
       . After the fall of
       <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">
        Assyria
       </a>
       between 616 BC and 605 BC, a unified Median state was formed, which together with Babylonia,
       <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">
        Lydia
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">
        ancient Egypt
       </a>
       became one of the four major powers of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">
        ancient Near East
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The Median kingdom was eventually conquered in 550 BC by
       <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">
        Cyrus the Great
       </a>
       , who established the
       <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
        Achaemenid Empire
       </a>
       . However, nowadays there is considerable doubt whether a united Median empire ever existed. There is no archaeological evidence and the story of
       <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">
        Herodotus
       </a>
       is not supported by sources from the
       <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">
        Neo-Assyrian Empire
       </a>
       nor the Neo-Babylonian Empire.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Median_dynasty">
        Median dynasty
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Medes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Median dynasty">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Median_Empire_Protoma.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1017" data-file-width="861" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Median_Empire_Protoma.jpg/180px-Median_Empire_Protoma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Median_Empire_Protoma.jpg/270px-Median_Empire_Protoma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Median_Empire_Protoma.jpg/360px-Median_Empire_Protoma.jpg 2x" width="180"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Median_Empire_Protoma.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Protoma in the form of a bull's head, 8th century BC, gold and filigree,
         <a href="/wiki/National_Museum,_Warsaw" title="National Museum, Warsaw">
          National Museum
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">
          Warsaw
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The list of Median rulers and their period of reign compiled according to two sources. Firstly, Herodotus who calls them "kings" and associates them with the same family. Secondly, the Babylonian Chronicle which in "Gadd's Chronicle on the Fall of Nineveh" gives its own list. A combined list stretching over 150 years is thus:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Deioces" title="Deioces">
         Deioces
        </a>
        (700–647 BC)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Phraortes" title="Phraortes">
         Phraortes
        </a>
        (647–625 BC)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">
         Scythian
        </a>
        rule (624–597 BC)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Cyaxares" title="Cyaxares">
         Cyaxares
        </a>
        (624–585 BC)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Astyages" title="Astyages">
         Astyages
        </a>
        (585–549 BC)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diakonoff1985_39-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Diakonoff1985-39">
          [37]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       However, not all of these dates and personalities given by Herodotus match the other near eastern sources.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diakonoff1985_39-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Diakonoff1985-39">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In Herodotus (book 1, chapters 95–130),
       <a href="/wiki/Deioces" title="Deioces">
        Deioces
       </a>
       is introduced as the founder of a centralised Median state. He had been known to the Median people as "a just and incorruptible man" and when asked by the Median people to solve their possible disputes he agreed and put forward the condition that they make him "king" and build a great city at Ecbatana as the capital of the Median state.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Judging from the contemporary sources of the region and disregarding
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the account of Herodotus puts the formation of a unified Median state during the reign of
       <a href="/wiki/Cyaxares" title="Cyaxares">
        Cyaxares
       </a>
       or later.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Culture_and_society">
        Culture and society
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Medes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Culture and society">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Greek references to "Median" people make no clear distinction between the "Persians" and the "Medians"; in fact for a Greek to become "too closely associated with Iranian culture" was "to become Medianized, not Persianized".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young-p449_28-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Young-p449-28">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Median kingdom was a short-lived Iranian state and the textual and archaeological sources of that period are rare and little could be known from the Median culture which nevertheless made a "profound, and lasting, contribution to the greater world of Iranian culture".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young-p450_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Young-p450-43">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Language">
        Language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Medes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Language">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Median_language" title="Median language">
        Median language
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Median people spoke the Median language, which was an Old Iranian language.
       <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">
        Strabo
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Geographica" title="Geographica">
         Geographica
        </a>
       </i>
       (finished in the early first century) mentions the affinity of Median with other
       <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">
        Iranian languages
       </a>
       : "The name of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ariana" title="Ariana">
         Ariana
        </a>
       </i>
       is further extended to a part of Persia and of Media, as also to the
       <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">
        Bactrians
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">
        Sogdians
       </a>
       on the north; for these speak approximately the same language, but with slight variations".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       No original deciphered text has been proven to have been written in the Median language. It is suggested that similar to the later Iranian practice of keeping archives of written documents in Achaemenid Iran, there was also a maintenance of archives by the Median government in their capital Ecbatana. There are examples of "Median literature" found in later records. One is according to Herodotus that the Median king Deioces, appearing as a judge, made judgement on causes submitted in writing. There is also a report by Dinon on the existence of "Median court poets".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Median literature is part of the "Old Iranian literature" (including also Saka, Old Persian, Avestan) as this Iranian affiliation of them is explicit also in ancient texts, such as Herodotus's account
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herodotus-vii-62_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Herodotus-vii-62-21">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       that many peoples including Medes were "universally called Iranian".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Words of Median origin appear in various other Iranian dialects, including
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">
        Old Persian
       </a>
       . A feature of Old Persian inscriptions is the large number of words and names from other languages and the Median language takes in this regard a special place for historical reasons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmitt2008-p98_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schmitt2008-p98-47">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Median words in Old Persian texts, whose Median origin can be established by "phonetic criteria",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmitt2008-p98_47-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schmitt2008-p98-47">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       appear "more frequently among royal titles and among terms of the chancellery, military, and judicial affairs".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmitt2008-p98_47-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schmitt2008-p98-47">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Words of Median origin include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         *čiθra-
        </i>
        : "origin".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
         <a href="#cite_note-48">
          [46]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The word appears in
        <i>
         *čiθrabṛzana-
        </i>
        (med.) "exalting his linage",
        <i>
         *čiθramiθra-
        </i>
        (med.) "having mithraic origin",
        <i>
         *čiθraspāta-
        </i>
        (med.) "having a brilliant army", etc.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
         <a href="#cite_note-49">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Farnah
        </i>
        : Divine glory (
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avestan_language" title="Avestan language">
         Avestan
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         <span lang="ae" xml:lang="ae">
          <a href="/wiki/Khvarenah" title="Khvarenah">
           khvarənah
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Paridaiza
        </i>
        :
        <a href="/wiki/Paradise" title="Paradise">
         Paradise
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Spaka-
        </i>
        : The word is Median and means "dog".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
         <a href="#cite_note-50">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Herodotus identifies "Spaka-" (Gk. "σπάχα" – female dog) as Median rather than Persian.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
         <a href="#cite_note-51">
          [49]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The word is still used in modern Iranian languages including
        <a href="/wiki/Talysh_language" title="Talysh language">
         Talyshi
        </a>
        , also suggested as a source to the Russian word for dog sobaka.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
         <a href="#cite_note-52">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
         <a href="#cite_note-53">
          [51]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
         <a href="#cite_note-54">
          [52]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         vazṛka-
        </i>
        : "great" (as
        <a href="/wiki/Western_Persian" title="Western Persian">
         Western Persian
        </a>
        <i>
         bozorg
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmitt2008-p98_47-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-Schmitt2008-p98-47">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         vispa-
        </i>
        : "all"
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
         <a href="#cite_note-55">
          [53]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (as in
        <a href="/wiki/Avestan" title="Avestan">
         Avestan
        </a>
        ). The component appears in such words as
        <i>
         vispafryā
        </i>
        (Med. fem.) "dear to all",
        <i>
         vispatarva-
        </i>
        (med.) "vanquishing all",
        <i>
         vispavada-
        </i>
        (Median-Old Persian) "leader of all", etc.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
         <a href="#cite_note-56">
          [54]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%90%8F%8B#Old_Persian" title="wikt:𐏋">
          xšayaθiya-
         </a>
        </i>
        (king)
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         xšaθra-
        </i>
        (realm; kingship): This Median word (attested in
        <i>
         *xšaθra-pā-
        </i>
        and continued by Middle Persian
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%90%AD%B1%F0%90%AD%B2%F0%90%AD%AB%F0%90%AD%A9#Middle_Persian" title="wikt:𐭱𐭲𐭫𐭩">
         <i>
          šahr
         </i>
        </a>
        "land, country; city") is an example of words whose Greek form (known as romanized "
        <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">
         satrap
        </a>
        " from Gk. σατράπης
        <i>
         satrápēs
        </i>
        ) mirrors, as opposed to the tradition,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
         <a href="#cite_note-58">
          [N 3]
         </a>
        </sup>
        a Median rather than an Old Persian form (also attested, as
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%90%8E%A7%F0%90%8F%81%F0%90%8F%82%F0%90%8E%B6#Old_Persian" title="wikt:𐎧𐏁𐏂𐎶">
          xšaça-
         </a>
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%90%8E%A7%F0%90%8F%81%F0%90%8F%82%F0%90%8E%B1%F0%90%8E%A0%F0%90%8E%BA%F0%90%8E%A0#Old_Persian" title="wikt:𐎧𐏁𐏂𐎱𐎠𐎺𐎠">
          xšaçapāvā
         </a>
        </i>
        ) of an Old Iranian word.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schmitt2008-p99_57-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-schmitt2008-p99-57">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         zūra-
        </i>
        : "evil" and
        <i>
         zūrakara-
        </i>
        : "evil-doer".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmitt2008-p98_47-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-Schmitt2008-p98-47">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">
        Religion
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Medes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Religion">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There are very limited sources concerning the religion of Median people. Primary sources pointing to religious affiliations of Medes found so far include the archaeological discoveries in Tepe Nush-e Jan, personal names of Median individuals, and the Histories of Herodotus. The archaeological source gives the earliest of the temple structures in Iran and the "stepped fire altar" discovered there is linked to the common Iranian legacy of the "cult of fire". Herodotus mentions
       <a href="/wiki/Magi#In_Iranian_sources" title="Magi">
        Median Magi
       </a>
       as a Median tribe providing priests for both the Medes and the Persians. They had a "priestly caste" which passed their functions from father to son. They played a significant role in the court of the Median king Astyages who had in his court certain Medians as "advisers, dream interpreters, and soothsayers". Classical historians "unanimously" regarded the Magi as priests of the Zoroastrian faith. From the personal names of Medes as recorded by Assyrians (in 8th and 9th centuries BC) there are examples of the use of the Indo-Iranian word
       <i>
        arta-
       </i>
       (lit. "truth") which is familiar from both Avestan and Old Persian and also examples of
       <a href="/wiki/Theophoric_name" title="Theophoric name">
        theophoric names
       </a>
       containing
       <i>
        Maždakku
       </i>
       and also the name "Ahura Mazdā".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIR-MediaReligion_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-EIR-MediaReligion-59">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Scholars disagree whether these are indications of Zoroastrian religion amongst the Medes.
       <a href="/wiki/Diakonoff" title="Diakonoff">
        Diakonoff
       </a>
       believes that "Astyages and perhaps even Cyaxares had already embraced a religion derived from the teachings of Zoroaster" which was not identical with doctrine of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zarathustra" title="Zarathustra">
        Zarathustra
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mary_Boyce" title="Mary Boyce">
        Mary Boyce
       </a>
       believes that "the existence of the Magi in Media with their own traditions and forms of worship was an obstacle to Zoroastrian proselytizing there".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIR-MediaReligion_59-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-EIR-MediaReligion-59">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Boyce wrote that the Zoroastrian traditions in the Median city of Ray probably goes back to the 8th century BC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is suggested that from the 8th century BC, a form of "Mazdaism with common Iranian traditions" existed in Media and the strict
       <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">
        reforms of Zarathustra
       </a>
       began to spread in western Iran during the reign of the last Median kings in the 6th century BC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIR-MediaReligion_59-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-EIR-MediaReligion-59">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It has also been suggested
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch">
          <span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2016)">
           by whom?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       that
       <a href="/wiki/Mithra" title="Mithra">
        Mithra
       </a>
       is a Median name and Medes may have practised
       <a href="/wiki/Mithraism" title="Mithraism">
        Mithraism
       </a>
       and had Mithra as their supreme deity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Fall">
        Fall
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Medes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fall">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hamadan_(Iran)_Relief_Achamenid_Period.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Hamadan_%28Iran%29_Relief_Achamenid_Period.JPG/250px-Hamadan_%28Iran%29_Relief_Achamenid_Period.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Hamadan_%28Iran%29_Relief_Achamenid_Period.JPG/375px-Hamadan_%28Iran%29_Relief_Achamenid_Period.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Hamadan_%28Iran%29_Relief_Achamenid_Period.JPG/500px-Hamadan_%28Iran%29_Relief_Achamenid_Period.JPG 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hamadan_(Iran)_Relief_Achamenid_Period.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <i>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganj_Nameh" title="Ganj Nameh">
           Ganj Nameh
          </a>
         </i>
         ("treasure epistle") in Ecbatana. The inscriptions are by Darius I and his son in Xerxes I
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1284" data-file-width="1908" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg/250px-Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg/375px-Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg/500px-Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Persepolis_The_Persian_Soldiers.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Apadana Hall, 5th century BC
         <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
          Achaemenid-era
         </a>
         carving of Persian and Median soldiers in traditional costume (Medians are wearing rounded hats and boots), in
         <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">
          Persepolis
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">
          Iran
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">
        Achaemenid Assyria
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 553 BC,
       <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">
        Cyrus the Great
       </a>
       , King of Persia, rebelled against his grandfather, the Mede King,
       <a href="/wiki/Astyages" title="Astyages">
        Astyages
       </a>
       son of Cyaxares; he finally won a decisive victory in 550 BC resulting in Astyages' capture by his own dissatisfied nobles, who promptly turned him over to the triumphant Cyrus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After Cyrus's victory against Astyages, the Medes were subjected to their close kin, the Persians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the new empire they retained a prominent position; in honour and war, they stood next to the Persians; their court ceremony was adopted by the new sovereigns, who in the summer months resided in
       <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">
        Ecbatana
       </a>
       ; and many noble Medes were employed as officials,
       <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">
        satraps
       </a>
       and generals.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Kurds_and_Medes">
        Kurds and Medes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Medes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Kurds and Medes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_the_Kurds" title="Origin of the Kurds">
        Origin of the Kurds
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Russian historian and linguist
       <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Minorsky" title="Vladimir Minorsky">
        Vladimir Minorsky
       </a>
       suggested that the Medes, who widely inhabited the land where currently the
       <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">
        Kurds
       </a>
       form a majority, might have been forefathers of the modern Kurds. He also states that the Medes who invaded the region in the eighth century BC, linguistically resembled the Kurds. This view was accepted by many Kurdish nationalists in the twentieth century. However,
       <a href="/wiki/Martin_van_Bruinessen" title="Martin van Bruinessen">
        Martin van Bruinessen
       </a>
       , a Dutch scholar, argues against the attempt to take the Medes as ancestors of the Kurds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ozoglu25_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ozoglu25-64">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        "Though some Kurdish intellectuals claim that their people are descended from the Medes, there is no evidence to permit such a connection across the considerable gap in time between the political dominance of the Medes and the first attestation of the Kurds" - van Bruinessen
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Contemporary linguistic evidence has challenged the previously suggested view that the Kurds are descendants of the Medes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gernot_Windfuhr&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gernot Windfuhr (page does not exist)">
        Gernot Windfuhr
       </a>
       (professor of Iranian Studies) identified the
       <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_languages" title="Kurdish languages">
        Kurdish languages
       </a>
       as
       <a href="/wiki/Parthian_language" title="Parthian language">
        Parthian
       </a>
       , albeit with a
       <a href="/wiki/Median_language" title="Median language">
        Median
       </a>
       substratum.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/David_Neil_MacKenzie" title="David Neil MacKenzie">
        David Neil MacKenzie
       </a>
       , an authority on the Kurdish language, said Kurdish was relatively [more] closer to Persian and questioned the “traditional” view holding that Kurdish, because of its differences from Persian, should be regarded a “NW-Iranian” language .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Paul-68">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Garnik Asatrian stated that "The Central Iranian dialects, and primarily those of the Kashan area in the first place, as well as the Azari dialects (otherwise called Southern Tati) are probably the only Iranian dialects, which can pretend to be the direct offshoots of Median ... In general, the relationship between Kurdish and Median are not closer than the affinities between the latter and other North Western dialects — Baluchi, Talishi, South Caspian, Zaza, Gurani, etc."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
